B005EGXTEE|3.0|Fascinated by the hype the film's opening generated, and the fact that my 13 year old (boy) stated 'the books are the best and I cant wait to see the film' I decided to find out myself what all the fuss is about.<br />Three books later, and after several intense conversations with those (adults) who have seen the film (I have not), I find myself faced with the dilemma - Is this review of a childrens' series? Is it a review of a political and social veiled commentary? Is it a review of an adults series? I am unsure. The story centers around a 'country/world' (it is never clear), where a ruthless regime (aspects of which bear a striking resemblance to North Korea, Burma and recent events in Syria), maintain control through fear, manipulation, cruelty and hunger. However adults in this story play either a villianous or secondary role, children are the regimes main targets, source of opposition, heros (perhaps) and victims.<br /><br />As an adult and a parent I found this both distressing and confusing. Joseph Kony and other african warlords have obviously inspired the author.  But to the story! The evil regime having faced an uprising in the past has determined that every year each district must select two children to represent them in The Hunger Games. The world of the Hunger Games is divided into the 'Capitol' (evil, controlling, home of the president, materialistic and most definately not hungry) and the districts each with a singular purpose (farming, mining, manufacturing) that have varying population levels and are portrayed as basically concentration camps where the population is starved, beaten and controlled by the ruthless armies (peacekeepers) of the 'Capitol'. The games themselves are gladitorial. Each child goes into a man made world with the sole aim of killing every other child, and thus achieving both victory, survival and food. Of course none of this can happen until each child is groomed and paraded around to the citizens of the capitol, who obviouslyhave no issue with media manipulation, child abuse and child murder. Oliver Stone in Natural Born Killers parodied a similiar media obsession.<br /><br />Our protagonist 'Katniss' is a 17 year old female from District 12, portrayed as one of the hungriest camps, in fact Katniss' greatest skill is creeping out of the confines of her camp to hunt successfully (with a bow and arrow) for food, many in her camp/district are in her debt for keeping them alive. Obviously Katniss is selected for the games. I wont give away the ending in the first book or in fact at the end of the third but it is as unexpected as expected. Themes of hunger are well represented and food and its consumption fill great numbers of pages, as do the author's annoying habit of spending many pages flashing back on Katniss' memories, the only purpose for this appears to be the desire to reiterate (over and over again) the rationale for her behaviour. Because despite the situation she finds herself in, the supporting characters and her past she is hard to like or understand.<br /><br />The Hunger Games if more subtle and better written could have been a truly great trilogy for adults. If fewer complex themes, more behavioural analysis, well rounded characters and a purposeful blackness and futility had been applied it could have been a series worth series academic and educational attention. It is neither, adults who have seen the film are appalled at the violence and premise (these adults are not wowsers). My own reaction? Shock. Shock that these books resonate with our children and shock that there has been no real discussion regarding the themes and events portrayed in them.

0345535421|5.0|As a general rule, I am not fond of fantasy \\"trilogies\\" which, regardless of whether they're three books, four books or more, are often driven more by commercial considerations than creative needs.  Many of these tales could be told in one volume but have been bloated to more because later volumes will have a built-in audience.  This is not the case with A Song of Ice and Fire, more popularly known as The Game of Thrones books.  This is a big story that requires several volumes and several thousand pages to tell.<br /><br />A Clash of Kings is the second volume in this saga, taking up where the preceding book (A Game of Thrones) left off.  The story opens with the appearance of a comet.  It appears to be an omen, but of what?  Each side in the civil war that is erupting has its own interpretation.<br /><br />The central family in this story is the Starks.  At the beginning of the novel, Robb Stark, the teenage son of the late Stark patriach Ned, has become King of the North, at war with the \\"official\\" king Joffrey (backed by the Lannister clan) and the rival claimants to the throne, the previous king's brothers, Renly and Stannis.  Robb (who despite being a pivotal character, hardly appears in the book and has no chapters with his point-of-view) battles the Lannisters.<br /><br />Meanwhile, Stannis and Renly compete with each other, while Robb's mother Catelyn tries to broker alliances.  Meanwhile, Robb's sister Sansa is trapped in the capital city of King's Landing, the hostage of Joffrey's family.  Meanwhile, Robb's young brothers Bran and Rickon act as figurehead rulers of the Stark home of Winterfell.  Meanwhile, Robb's half-brother Jon explores the land beyond the Wall, where strange creatures dwell and bring the dead back as zombie killers.  Meanwhile, Theon Greyjoy, the former ward of the Starks tries to prove he's a worthy heir to his real father.  Meanwhile, Daenerys, the heir to a previous, usurped king, plots from exile her rise to power.  Meanwhile, a new monotheistic (and intolerant) religion threatens the polytheistic old ones (and shows some parallels to the rise of early Christianity).<br /><br />That's a lot of \\"meanwhiles\\" and I think I missed one or two.  This is a complex story and you would not want to start reading it with this volume.  But if you head volume one in this series and enjoyed it, you will be equally pleased with this one.

Shakespeare's Globe was reconstructred about 200 meters from the original site, and it was funded by an American, Sam Wanamaker (page 168).  What the guidebook fails to state, is that the money actually came from Samuel Scripps (1927-2007), who was a member of the Scripps family (Scripps Institute in San Diego, CA), and who had worked behind the scenes at various Shakespeare festivals in America, e.g., in Berkeley, Riverside, and New York.  The Imperial War Museum is detailed on page 276, where we learn that it is located in a former hospital, and that it includes a German V-2 rocket, a Sherman tank, spying equipment, and a Spitfire airplane. The book is not really divided into chapters, but instead, the book is divided into various sections, each from 2-pages long to about 20-pages long, having titles such as: (1) The West End; (2) The City; (3) The South Bank; (4) Kensington & Hyde Park; (5) Clerkenwell; (6) Shoreditch & Spitalfields; (7) The East End & Docklands; and so on.<br /><br />To sample a bit of the writing, here is a quote about a hotel called ROOKERY (page 322): \\"This absolute charmer is a warren of 33 rooms that has been built within a row of 18th century Georgian houses and fitted out with antique furniture, including original wood paneling, statues in bathrooms, and artworks, there's a small courtyard garden.\\"<br /><br />The most appropriate part of this guidebook, and one that distinguishes this guidebook from most other guidebooks, is the TIMELINE (page 344-361).  The book needs a timeline, and it does in fact have a timeline, because the science, technology, and law of much of the Western civilization was derived from innovations coming from England.  (To give one small example, PATENT LAW, in its modern form, came from the Statute of Monolpolies, and English statute from 1623.)  The TIMELINE provides highlights from the years 43AD to 2011BC, and we learn about the Romans, King Alfred the Great, William the Conqueror, King John (the villain of the Robin Hood stories, and source of inspiration for the MAGNACARTA, presently displayed at BRITISH LIBRARY).  BRITISH LIBRARY is detailed on pages 235-236, and we learn that it also contains a real Gutenberg Bible, Captain Scott's diary, manuscripts by Thomas Hardy, Shakespeare, Lewis Carrol, original scores by Beethoven, and handwritten lyrics by The Beatles.  The TIMELINE also shows Westminster Abbey, built during the time of William the Conqueror's reign.  The TIMELINE has small photos corresponding to many of the events or buildings erected, as history unfolded itself.  WESTMINSTER ABBEY is detailed on pages 80-82, and we see a half-page photo of the building, and learn that most of the kings of England were crowned here.  Its architecture is \\"Early English Gothic\\" (1180-1280).  The guidebook also tells us that the grounds of the ABBEY include, e.g., Chapter House, Pyx Chamber, Abbey Museum, Poet's Corner, and Isaac Newton's Tomb.  The TIMELINE also discloses the 45-year reign of Elizabeth I (a time when religeous tolerance began to take hold), and when the British defeated the Spanish Armada (1588), and we learn the dates of the great plagues (1348 & 1665) and of the Great Fire (1666).  The potential tourist who reads the timeline, will hopefully be inspired to purchase a book on each of the topics disclosed, and in this way, will be able to actually understand the buildings, palaces, and monuments, that are viewed during his or her vacation in London.<br /><br />The TIMELINE (page 357) shows a small picture of ST.PAUL'S CATHEDRAL, noted for surviving Nazi bombing. 0439023521|3.0|Before I begin, I'll be the first to admit that I was expecting a lot, going into this. Unfortunately, it just... wasn't.<br /><br />*The Hunger Games* is split into three parts: \\"The Tributes,\\" \\"The Games,\\" and \\"The Victor.\\" (Note: while there are also three novels in the series, I am speaking only of the first novel, which is itself divided into the aforementioned parts.) What's interesting is that while many books increase in intensity from start to finish, this one is more like a bell curve. Part I (comprising chapters 1-9) begins a bit slowly, as it necessarily lays the groundwork for the story ahead. This, of course, is to be expected, and by the end of Part I, I had come to know and love the main protagonist. As Part II (chapters 10-18) begins, I was finally fully engaged in the story, and I loved every minute of it, constantly clamoring for more.<br /><br />But then I got to Part III.<br /><br />I'm not sure what was going through Suzanne Collins' mind as she wrote the third and concluding part of this novel, but it is, in a word, boring. It is so bad that there were several times I was about to close the book for good, and just not waste my time seeing how it ended. Still, I persevered, confident that since so many people think it's so great, there had to be something worthwhile at the end, something that would make this tremendous monotony worth it. The problem is: there isn't.<br /><br />After slogging through almost 100 pages of boredom, I finally arrived at the climax of the novel, only to be presented with a ridiculous, contrived, final battle that wasn't even exciting while it happened. And then, once it was finally over and I got to enjoy the results, there are still another 30 pages of slogging boredom before I finally read \\"END OF BOOK ONE\\"--as if I'd actually subject myself to two more novels of this.<br /><br />I realize, of course, that I'm not in the \\"young adult\\" target audience, but I enjoy several other novels of that genre. \\"Young Adult\\" doesn't necessarily mean \\"catering to tweenage pop culture,\\" but the climax was definitely just that.<br /><br />I'd also like to point out one of my three stars is because *The Hunger Games* is completely and utterly devoid of adult language and sexual situations, a feature I wish were found in more of modern literature. Even one of my favorite books of all time, *Ender's Game*, doesn't have that feature.<br /><br />So, all in all, a book that really failed to impress, but that I might read again, someday, at least in part. I'd still love to know what other people find so compelling, and maybe I'll find it on the second reading.

I found, in a tiny shop in my home city, this and Uomo di Pezza soon afterwards. So different. Later, I was tempted to buy the English language version, but as an impecunious student, thought better of it. Forty years later, that decision was vindicated. What induced them to get Peter Hammill to write some unrelated gobbledigook, and append it to the instrumentals?  Heavens to Betsy...Anglophones have listened to non English vocals for centuries. This offering contains both versions.<br /><br />The original Felona e Sorona is mostly a sublime dip into what Italian Prog could produce at its best. This dusting off  is excellent, and sounds much as I recall it. Crisp production, good sonic balance, and ambience, and the work itself all fits together.<br /><br />Le Orme were keyboard and percussion based, though the small guitar parts are perfectly competent. However, unlike ELP and other Keys based Prog outfits, the keys don't overwhelm to the point of distraction. I don't care that Keith E played with the Hammond lying on him...it so often ceased to be musical! I had had an Italian mate translate the lyrics, but he found them somewhat arcane. I prefer the metaphysical to the Hammill inanities. Especially as so often their struggle with English interrupted the flow of the vocals.<br />The music here is at once seductive, beckoning, contemplative at times and rollicks along at others, but always the music had a legitimate reason to be.<br />The English, Felona and Sorona, is just daft. Similar comments can be directed at other misguided meldings of English Language lyrics with  Italian prog instrumentals.<br />I found it distracting enough.<br /><br />So...the Italian language original is a must have for Proggers, but the English version is a historical curiosity only.

0312641893|4.0|For full review, please visit: [...]    Imagine R2-D2 and C-3PO combing the streets of a Chinese bazaar. Imagine that handsome and charming prince you so often dreamt of as a child. And imagine Cinderella as a girl with a metal hand and foot...because she's simply a cyborg living as a mechanic in New Beijing. Cinder was so ridiculously odd that it was...well... ridiculously awesome. Never would have I ever imagined a Cinderella cyborg with an Asian prince -  I absolutely loved that concept! Loved.    This Cinderella retelling is simply charming.  It's  very lighthearted and fun -  very magical and very fairy tale-ish. Cinder is a great character...level-headed, independent and smart. She's got a tomboyish feature to her, which is something I found very suitable for the role she played. She's a very serious gal and surprisingly it worked for this storyline without being a bore. This paired with her adorable crush on the prince, and Cinder's got to be one of the cutest characters ever when in the presence of royalty.  I really liked her as our heroine but I also loved Prince Kai as our prince charming. He was simply sweet and gallant in every way. Usually, I'm not the biggest fan of love interests or male counterparts that are too perfect or too nice. But somehow...Marissa Meyer's brilliancy managed to make those two traits extremely sexy on Prince Kai. I was practically fanning myself during the elevator scene between  him and Cinder. Propped up against the elevator, asking her to the ball...whew! No wonder he's a heartthrob in New Beijing. I can assure you, Prince Kai is one of the few love interests from a YA fantasy novel that will make you swoon while still maintaining proper etiquette - him and Jem Carstairs of course.  Though I'm a fan of Prince Kai and I loved reading from his perspective,  I'm kind of glad that we never really get an insight on how he felt for Cinder. All the more agonizing once you reach the end of this book!    I felt the world building wasvery unique. I loved the oriental elements that were included, though I can't be sure if they were all Chinese.  Nevertheless, I felt readers were given enough information to understand how Meyer's world functioned. Honestly, as much as I might be a stickler for realistic plots, I don't need a whole history lesson to understand how the world got to where it was. Questioning everything seems to ruin the fun for me at times - so honestly, I don't need to know every political change or detail that happened before Cinder's story began. I just need to know the basics to understand that somehow the world ended up the way that Meyer created it (with the exception that the plot makes sense and isn't too ridiculously out there - see Waterfall review). That said, I felt the world building behind Cinder was appropriate for the writing style that was conveyed.    The only thing I did question in Cinder was the pacing  - in respect to the romance and timeline of events. We lost a few characters in this book, but no one really took the time to mourn their loss, which I felt really took away from the emotional aspect of the story. The big reveal was also pretty obvious from the start - from the moment it was first mentioned I knew... Regardless, I thought Meyer did an excellent job overall and I'm eager to find out what happens to Cinder in her next book of the series. My biggest fear with the Lunar Chronicles series, is the introduction of new main characters for the remaining books. I am absolutely obsessed with Cinder's story, so I'll admit that I hate change and will probably be very stubborn with the change of cast - regardless of whether or not Cinder makes minor appearances. I am, however, confident that Marissa Meyer will surprise me in the end. I just might actually enjoy the direction she takes with the Lunar Chronicles. Hopefully that's what will happen...hopefully.    The theme music for Cinder is In Reverence by David Tolk. Typically, I summarize books in my head with a music video, and so I thought this music went well with the images that I envisioned. Cinder staring out at New Beijing from the palace, Cinder repairing androids in the storage room, Cinder sad...you get the picture. Definitely check out Cinder - it's a delightful book to add to your bookshelf!

1401309666|5.0|First of all, if you are paging down to look at the reviews of this book to see if it might be worth reading, I recommend you do. This book is well worth a look for those interested in a relatively new and fresh perspective on the ideas of eCommerce and online retail like this very site.<br /><br />As for the book itself, The Long(er) Tail (2008) is the revised version of Anderson's 2006 book, with a new chapter focused on marketing. It is a quick read at just under 300 pages and never gets too dry or boring, even when it delves into the particulars of Anderson's theory.<br /><br />The concept of the Long Tail is taken from a sales graph of music downloads through Rhapsody. The \\"hits\\" make up a majority of sales profit for music downloads, but thanks to the internet and all of the retail technology at their disposal, Rhapsody has more than just the 2,000 most popular tracks available, they have a few million. And, if the downloads are graphed out, there is a trailing section of sales that never quite falls to zero, as even the most obscure tracks are downloaded at least a few times a month. Hence, the graph has a long tail.<br /><br />The concept of Long Tale economics, then, is that markets are no longer solely tied to the concept of \\"hits\\", of blockbusters and New York Times bestsellers to make profit. A company can turn a profit, and quite tidily, by focusing on the more obscure, the niche products, using the unlimited shelf space of the internet to stock millions of different products and making them available to as many people as possible.<br /><br />Anderson explores, expands, extrapolates and dissects this theory throughout the book, from discussing the history of the Long Tail market and the technology that makes it possible, to the new chapter on marketing and how web advertizing is being geared towards individualization of the customer, rather than mass-media marketing.<br /><br />As Anderson himself says, the Long Tail is by no means the only market out there, the \\"hits\\" market still exists and still has the lion's share of the sales figures. But for the first time since mass marketing was invented, the niche markets, where the products people purchase are what they want, rather than the products everyone else is buying, have a significant share in the market. And that share is only getting bigger.

B0073XORLU|4.0|It's not a Jethro Tull album. So okay, I'll buy no Martin Barre. He was good not being on Rupi's Dance (as would I). I'll also buy that Ian has a gigantic ego. Who knew? That said, who'da thunk I'd be writing a review for any release (that wasn't a Christmas album) by this dude at this point? I've enjoyed Jethro Tull for a long time, but really gave up on them in the late 70's. Last album I bought was Stormwatch, last tour I saw too. Eddie Jobson's entrance wasn't enough to sway me back to Ian's world after that point. Any taste I had of Ian's subsequent outings be they fishy or feline were met by me with indifference.<br /><br />So, with this Ian Anderson release what do we have? If you were to have heard it blind a few months ago you'd swear it was an outtake from some session past. Great lengths have been taken to replicate the Hammond organ and acoustic/electric guitar sound of TAAB. Also present are Ian's voice, which while nothing like it was in the 70's is in the best form it's been in years. He's not rattling off verses at 4/4 time anymore, and everything is written well within his range. It's all just a little more laid back. He still goes from soft and lilting to loud and distorted (with a little technical help) and back again. Of course his flute playing is everything it ever was, and is never enough.<br /><br />I'm sure you're already familiar with the concept of the album, suffice to say it's a really clever way of approaching old subject matter. Ian has always been masterful at third person storytelling, but this is quite a departure thematically. It's really all quite involving. Spoken word passages bookend the \\"chapters\\" throughout. A lot of his biting, bitter wit and social commentary is seen through slightly softer jaded eyes. Ian doesn't really care what anybody thinks of him or anything else for that matter anymore.<br /><br />Also present and long missed are actual melodies. Ear worm stuff. Perhaps some of these tunes have been rattling around for a while, but I hope not. I'm wanting more! While the songs have a more individual nature than TAAB, the album flows nicely as a cohesive unit. There are repeating themes as well as sections culled from TAAB for continuity, but it's nothing like I was imagining it would be. Many of these songs do have the TAAB feel to them as far as song structure. Ian seems to have channeled a lot of that \\"where he was at\\" at the time feeling. So well done in fact I'm beginning to mix up the two albums in my head!<br /><br />There are of course elements of many of his \\"periods\\" here, and while there's nothing particularly new, there's nothing that seems derivative either. Echoes of the rocking War Child and Minstrels, the pastoral sound of Songs and Horses as well as jazzy or eastern infused Roots and Rupi's all seem present. The song A Change of Horses embodies many of these in its 8 minute running time. Speaking of running time, this one clocks in at 53 minutes, longer than any of the classic albums. But it seems to fly. Seriously there are more than just a couple gems in here. The rest of the musicianship is wonderful, I won't expound on any elements as that's purely subjective. I leave that to you.<br /><br />Now to voice my complaints. At times it's all just a little too slick. I'm not really getting a sense of \\"chemistry\\" here. It could be it's just Ian having complete control over the process and production. There are also points where Ian himself sounds as if he's phoning it in, but then again he's seemed that way in previous efforts at times as well. Replicating the enchantment of past Tull albums I would assume to be an impossible task regardless. The studio itself is now a sterile high-tech environment, and a cocoon from outside influences. Gone are the days of crappy borrowed analog equipment and all night sessions fueled by cigarettes, alcohol and - \\"whatever\\". And with the new perfected environment a bit of the magic dies I suppose.<br /><br />If you're reading thisyou probably have a pretty strong feeling one way or the other about the album, the band, the man. But in the end, Jethro Tull always was about Ian, he was just really good at dragging the rest along for the ride. Of course I'm sorry that some got off at a different stop. As a solo album I'd say it's pretty darn good - dare I say even inspired. As a Jethro Tull album - it more than stands up. Apparently, having a story to tell makes all the difference and Ian has something to say. Tell me more! I for one am happy to take a bite from the classic prog apple after 30 some odd years. This doesn't in any way feel like an old vet trying fly from here to recapture former glories. It sounds fresh and vintage at the same time. Like a friend you haven't seen for ages. Once you talk it's like no time at all has passed as you relive the past, and yet everything has changed. As Ian says, \\"Odd chap, life\\".<br /><br />4 easy \\"I like it\\" Amazon Stars (really almost 5 just because). But 3 if you're one of those music reviewer types. Sound quality is a solid 4 if not better, this is probably the best sound ever for JT or IA - thanks (again) Steve! This one will be in rotation for a long while!you probably have a pretty strong feeling one way or the other about the album, the band, the man. 0756405890|4.0|When I picked up The Name of the Wind, I knew 3 things about it:<br /><br />1. It is an \\"epic fantasy.\\"<br /><br />2. The protagonist is an innkeeper.<br /><br />3. It's supposed to be awesome.<br /><br />Now, there's something you need to know about me and things that are supposed to be awesome: I tend to not think they're awesome. I'll spare you specific examples [*cough*Gone With the Wind*cough] so that you don't freak out and decide to hate me forever. I just tend to like what I like, and what I like often doesn't line up with what the world at large likes.<br /><br />Plus, the more something is hyped, the more disappointed I tend to be when it's not all I imagined it would be. You'd think I'd learn my lesson and stop expecting greatness every time greatness is forecast, but no. I'm incorrigible that way.<br /><br />So I went into The Name of the Wind in a divided state. On the one hand, I was really excited to read a spectacularly awesome fantasy book. On the other, I was bracing myself for disappointment when I inevitably disagreed with the populace at large.<br /><br />And after finishing TNOTW, I have to say...I'm still divided.<br /><br />THE PLOT<br /><br />TNOTW revolves around Kote, keeper of a modest inn in a small town. As the story opens, the townsfolk are all in a tizzy over some strange happenings: namely, giant spider-demons attacking travelers on the road. They come into the inn, troubled and flummoxed, and discuss the horrors of the world outside.<br /><br />Then, unbeknownst to the townsfolk, Kote and his assistant, Bast, have a Very Mysterious Discussion where we come to realize that Kote knows something about the spider-demons, and that for some reason (beyond the obvious fact that they are giant spider-demons), they are Very Bad.<br /><br />Then he goes out to kill a bunch of them. But before he can, he stumbles into a traveling scribe. Or rather, the scribe -- Chronicler -- stumbles into him. Chronicler was riding down the road, minding his own business, when he was robbed by highwaymen and forced to set out on foot. Lucky for him, he wandered straight into where Kote had set up spider-killing camp, and arrived just in time for the party.<br /><br />After witnessing Kote single-handedly dispatch a bunch of spider-demons, and subsequently passing out, Chronicler is brought to the inn, where the truth quickly comes out: Kote is really Kvothe (pronounced \\"quothe,\\" and thus screwing with my internal narrator every single time I read his name), the legendary -- and presumed dead -- hero. Chronicler immediately presses Kvothe for information, since he's pretty much the most famous guy ever. And Kvothe grudgingly agrees to tell him the story of his life. It will take 3 days.<br /><br />[Side note: It took me for-e-ver to realize that the book's subtitle, \\"The Kingkiller Chronicle: Day 1,\\" refers to the first day of storytelling. Duh.]<br /><br />Thus begins the story of Kvothe's childhood, where he was raised by loving parents in a troupe of traveling performers...until they were brutally murdered by the Chandrain, terrifying beings that, up until that point, Kvothe (and everybody else in the world) presumed didn't exist.<br /><br />Young Kvothe mourns his parents and friends, while struggling to survive, for several years before he sets off determined to complete his education at the University, which is a lot like Hogwarts: The College Years. The students learn about magic in a variety of forms, although they don't call it magic. \\"Magic\\" is a term for ignorant country bumpkins. University students pursue sympathy, artificery, alchemy, and other fancy words that really just mean magic.<br /><br />Kvothe is 15 when he enters the University, several years younger than his youngest classmates, but his prodigious intelligence, coupled with his sharp wit and abundant charisma, help him quickly advance through the ranks. He makes a few close friends and more than a few enemies. Oh, and there's a girl. Of course.<br /><br />MY THOUGHTS:<br /><br />I learned something about myself while reading this book. I realized that I am a big fan of the 3-act narrative.<br /><br />Act 1: Characters are introduced. Background is given. World is built.<br /><br />Act 2: Things go well. Hint of conflict. Or maybe even lots of conflict. You get a good feeling for who the good guys and bad guys are, and where the story is headed.<br /><br />Act 3: Big finish. Possibly a battle. Major conflict is resolved in a thrilling way. Possible set-up for future conflict if it isn't a stand-alone novel.<br /><br />Part of the reason I'm so divided about TNOTW is that it doesn't follow this formula. At all. Really, the whole book seems like a big Act 1. Maybe a bit of Act 2. Definitely no Act 3. I didn't realize that I was hungering for an Act 3 until I finished the book and never got one. I was so sure it would be there, even when I noticed I was down to the last 50 pages and no noticeable conflict was being set up, that I was actually shocked when it never happened.<br /><br />But then again, this book never promised me that it would be set up in 3 acts. As a matter of fact, it told me very clearly what to expect when Kvothe began his tale: a story of a man's life that would be told in 3 days. And this was Day 1. The fact that I didn't really pick up on this -- or was expecting the story of his life to fall neatly into three days of 3-act narratives -- is my fault.<br /><br />So I need to judge this book on what it is, and not what I expected it to be.<br /><br />The Pros:<br /><br />Mr. Rothfuss is a wonderful storyteller. He gives enough detail to draw a reader into the world, but not so much that I got bored or that my attention wandered. He was able to draw from me every emotional response that the story required: happiness, contentment, sadness, fear, desperation, determination, anger, triumph.<br /><br />Probably one of my favorite scenes in the whole book was Kvothe's first time performing at the Eolian. As a musician and former performer, I could vividly empathize with all the emotions he was feeling, and I actually felt my heart racing when he took the stage. It was a magnificent scene, even though (as far as I can tell) it had nothing to do with Kvothe eventually becoming a hero. Being able to draw me so far into scenes like that -- scenes with little adventure or intrigue, but full of emotion -- was one of the book's great strengths.<br /><br />The magical world he created was also extremely well-developed. The laws of sympathy are well-defined and loosely rooted in science, which makes it almost hard to believe that this stuff couldn't work in real life. All the divisions of sympathy work together in an orderly and understandable way. And Mr. Rothfuss never bends or breaks the laws of sympathy, even when it could possibly make for some cool action scenes.<br /><br />Kvothe's time at the University is in turns exciting, frustrating, illuminating, and captivating. And always interesting.<br /><br />The Cons:<br /><br />First off, many of the main characters have more than one name (maybe this is meant to underscore the heavy emphasis placed on Naming in the story). Kvothe, Bast, Chronicler, Denna...all go by multiple names during the course of the story. It's not really confusing, but I'm also not sure it's entirely necessary. Kvothe/Kote's name change is understandable. I don't know if the rest really are.<br /><br />Secondly, Kvothe is kind of irritating. I didn't dislike him, but I didn't love him either. He's just too good at everything. He's ridiculously handsome. He has a voice like an angel. He can play the lute like he's some sort of superhuman with 15 fingers. He's apparently memorized every minute fact to have ever tripped across his consciousness. He excels at every form of sympathy that he tries. He can spin a yarn, work a crowd, woo the ladies, and outmaneuver the wiliest of weasels.<br /><br />Eventually, you just kind of want a protagonistto have some weaknesses, ya know? And no, \\"I'm misunderstood by the jealous and petty professor/student because they're envious of my awesomeness\\" is not weakness.<br /><br />Lastly, I felt at times the story lacked direction. It opens with the Very Mysterious and Terrifying spider-demons, then never mentions them again until the very end of the book, and then only kind of. It sets up Kvothe to be the greatest and most famous hero the world has ever known, but doesn't even begin to tell us why. He spends the entire book trying to find information about the Chandrain, and ends with barely more than he started with.<br /><br />I understand that most of this is probably because this is the first book in a trilogy, but I'm of the opinion that if you can't resolve any of the story lines in the first book, then you should just write one long book. Overarching plots and conflicts are great. I am a fan of the multi-book series. But let's resolve something in book 1. Let's not just write trilogies for trilogies' sake. I know trilogies are all the rage right now, but come on. Let's have a purpose.<br /><br />A friend described the middle of the book as \\"meandering,\\" and I'd have to agree. It meanders. It's an interesting meander. I never was bored, or annoyed. But I couldn't figure out what the point of all these little vignettes was. It just kind of moseyed around, taking its sweet time, with no clear destination in mind.<br /><br />What's more, since the story never really felt like it was going anywhere or driving toward anything, I never really had that sense of urgency to keep my turning pages. I could put the book down in the middle of a chapter and not feel that pull -- you know, the one. It's the one you feel when you're doing dishes or folding the laundry, and all of a sudden you're not doing those things anymore because you're reading. You didn't decide, necessarily, to stop being productive and start reading. You just were reading, because the book pulled you back. Because there was something in the story that hadn't resolved, and you needed to know how it resolved.<br /><br />Anyway. I never felt the pull. I would enjoy the book while I was reading, put it down when I needed to, and pick it up later when I remembered I was in the middle of a book. It was a weird feeling to be able to completely walk away from a book I was actually enjoying, and not feel the pull. It's never happened to me before.<br /><br />THE VERDICT:<br /><br />So I'm torn. Was this book an \\"epic fantasy\\" in the way I'm used to thinking of epic fantasies? No. Yes, the hero goes on a journey, but nothing much is accomplished, and only a few years of Kvothe's early life are covered. Maybe the trilogy as a whole will be an epic fantasy. But as far as I'm concerned, TNOTW is simply a fantasy. Nothing epic about it.<br /><br />Was it enjoyable? Yes, for the most part. Frustrating when I realized I was basically just reading one big setup for book 2 (or maybe even book 3), and wasn't going to get any payoff in this book. But barring that, it was enjoyable.<br /><br />Was it well-written? Did everything in the book make sense, according to the rules of the world Kvothe was living in? Absolutely. Zero complaints there.<br /><br />Do I recommend it? Right now I'm going to say yes. I haven't finished book 2 (The Wise Man's Fear) yet, and book 3 (The Doors of Stone) hasn't even been released. I have a feeling this is one of those trilogies where once I finish the whole thing, it's going to drastically impact my opinion of the first book. But for now, I'm going to say yes. It has great potential to be a fantastic story.<br /><br />I just hope something actually happens in the next book.Eolian. 0805079831|5.0|A sickening read describing how Milton Friedman's economic theories, put into reality by primarily Republican Party members such as the second President George Bush, have consistently produced failing States instead. Iraq is one example the author discusses with plenty of evidence how by outsourcing government war and the following 'nation building' by using corporation employees in place of government employees, we promoted the theft of billions of taxpayer money through inflated billings while incompetent contractors look as if they are doing something to fulfill their contracts. Klein also shows how other governments, in trying to save themselves from a variety of economic crises, employ Friedman's horrible policies, imposed on them by various international powers and banks, end up impoverishing the citizens of those countries while foreigners and homegrown elites end up extracting most of the wealth created to their own bank accounts. The shock doctrine is forcing a country to sell off most of its assets by taking advantage of natural disasters or intentionally created financial crashes which bring a country to its knees. When a country is faced with a sudden disaster where many people are suddenly out of work and governments are stunned by the suffering and fear and destruction, banks and corporations move in and rape the country while promising to fix the problems with cash borrowed at high interest, imposing strict limitations on social security, fuel supports or food subsidies to the poor. The banks also force the sale of publicly owned electrical grids and water and sewer plants to private foreign companies and countries, who then fire the local employees and raise the price of the heretofore affordable necessities of daily life. Poverty rates typically jump from perhaps 10% to 30-60%. While the people are shocked by the disasters, they meekly allow the foreign corporations to steal their assets and starve them. Friedman promoted using the most extreme methods to deepen the shock and upset, through what used to be called brainwashing methods, which in actuality means torture of the kind used at Guantanamo in Cuba by the USA on the suspected Muslim terrorists, to destroy anyone who felt like protesting being put out of work and starving. In other words, a troubled country is forced into deeper, more painful difficulties intentionally in order to force the selling at rock bottom prices of everything of value to business entities which have no interest in helping or saving the country's citizens. Friedman thinks it will improve the functioning of everything eventually; however, Klein demonstrates in example after real world example (Brazil, Argentina, Iraq, Indonesia, Thailand, and others) how Friedman's theories put into practice destroyed the lives of millions of people and enriched only the top 1% , and along the way brought unbearable suffering.ods to deepen the shock and upset, through what used to be called brainwashing methods, which in actuality means torture of the kind used at Guantanamo in Cuba by the USA on the suspected Muslim terrorists, to destroy anyone who felt like protesting being put out of work and starving. B0074EIQUG|5.0|A ball of fire continues to reside within Dr. John, casting a poetic divergence between the artist and the title of his new set of tunes. \\"Locked Down\\" is a blazing fusion of highly literate, funky, southern-fried Americana, blues, pop, soul and R&B that shows the venerable New Orleans-born musician in full creative force, with each detail invigorating, precise and carefully-honed.<br /><br />One can only hope that Nonesuch (this marks Dr. John's debut on the label) will know how to market the album correctly. \\"Locked Down\\" has the power to please not only lifelong appreciators of his verve and dexterity but has the power to reel in new listeners as well.<br /><br />The life force that has Dr. John sounding remarkably invigorated at this later stage of his career has much to do with Dan Auerbach of the Black Keys, who assembled great players such as drummer Max Weissenfeldt and bassist Nick Movshon. They support him and this solid collection of tunes with passion and professionalism, causing the ingredients to slip into place like puzzle pieces.<br /><br />The fast-moving set of ten tunes sounds ripe for the 70s LP album market in more ways than one. \\"Getaway\\" pulses with longing, grit and a hurts-so-good desperation that is downright infectious. \\"Kingdom of Izzness\\" is downright groovy and chilly, as is the dark social commentary of \\"Ice Age,\\" while the entrancing poetry of the title track is anchored by steady percussion and hearty guitar licks.<br /><br />There is a lifetime of experience in his voice and playing. When Dr. John sings a line such as \\"Don't take the easy road - there's no short cut\\" from the quietly insistent \\"My Children, My Angels\\" it comes with a specific and heavy weight attached to it that is instantly resonant. Aspiring musicians who want to know what a lifetime of devotion to their craft could culminate in would do well to heed that advice and pay mind to the music which surrounds it.<br /><br />Dr. John is every bit as part and parcel of American music as Bob Dylan or Bruce Springsteen. He has always offered more than mere entertainment, and \\"Locked Down\\" makes it clear that he remains very much present and in full voice, articulating important truths for those wise enough to be listening. This is music made purely for the pleasure of the craft, and that pleasure is perfectly infectious.erican music as Bob Dylan or Bruce Springsteen. B000JMKQX0|5.0|Welcome to the zombie apocalypse. World War Z shows the progression of a zombie apocalypse through a series of interviews that explore the experiences of survivors from around the world. Each interview or chapter further develops the story to paint the larger picture of a worldwide catastrophic event. Zombie fans and those that like post apocalyptic fiction in general will enjoy this book. You could substitute \\"zombies\\" for any number of apocalyptic events. It just so happens that zombies are the perfect enemy. A zombie acts on instinct alone, has no fear or conscience and will never stop until their brain is destroyed. World War Z (WWZ) is unique in a number of ways. First of all, the story is conveyed through a series of interviews and shows a wide variety of perspectives from people with different cultures and geographic locations. Unlike most stories in the genre, World War Z is not confined to one geographic location. Max Brooks takes us all over the world from China, Russia, Britain, Siberia, US and even to the ocean's floor. Another unique feature of WWZ is that it covers the entire war, from the early stages of the outbreak right up until the end where humans develop efficient ways to destroy the zombie hordes and take back much of the planet. WWZ also proposes some interesting ideas. For example, government authorities conclude that they cannot save everyone and that in order to save some people, others must be sacrificed. Although one could criticize the way in which the author proposes that \\"government authorities\\" would find and implement a solution for the vast majority, the harsh, pragmatic decision is logical in the context of this post apocalyptic scenario. WWZ is a unique, well written and exciting tale that I highly recommend you add to your list of \\"must read\\" post apocalyptic fiction.

B005LAIH2W|3.0|Okay, everybody and their funkin' monkey has weighed in on this flick, so I might as well throw in my two shiny pennies, and see if I can ever get my wish for a reboot of the source material.<br /><br />Straight off, the film was not bad. It was a good little adventure. Fun...ish, but not amazing. It was not particularly well written, nor brilliantly acted, and for the amount of cash spent on the production, I was not terribly impressed with the production values.<br /><br />Most of you gushing out there in the 5 & 4 star reviews are not seeing the disappointment of the film. Not really. I don't see the Barsoom of the books, or much of subsequent visualizations of them over the last century. Sad really. Andrew Stanton and crew chose to ignore so much, (such as the Frazetta and Whelan depictions of Barsoom,) in the their art design that would have made this movie classic and timeless, but they ran on the hubris of their own artistic bearing, and the thought that there was an endless amount funds to make things right.<br /><br />I'm going to mention some films that created a world properly. Recently, (though I have issues with it as well) James Cameron's [[ASIN:B0044XV3QY Avatar]] put forth a visual like few films ever have. Regardless of the alien Dances With Wolves storyline, the look and feel of the film was gripping. So to with Peter Jackson's [[ASIN:B000654ZK0 The Lord of the Rings]] trilogy, and it was fairly faithful to the source material, and had many powerful and evocative scenes throughout all three films. Riddley Scott's [[ASIN:B000K15VSA Blade Runner]], even after decades, and much controversy and drama in the production phase still stands up, and is as epic as it is dystopian. I could list other films, but these should suffice as converse exemplars of the problems within John Carter.<br /><br />And this is all said with a great sadness, because I wanted so much for this film to be better than it was.<br /><br />First, just to get it out of the way, the title sucked. Disney execs and Stanton should be ashamed. Secondly, the marketing sucked. There was no build up to the film with media and merchandising. Unbelievable. If Disney had done either of those properly, they actually might have made their numbers to make this a success. As it stands, as of the date of this review, the movie worldwide has made over $250 million. I feel it could have been a lot more, and thus there might have been sequels.<br /><br />So, let me get on with the scathing of the film.<br /><br />The art and style of the film was not horrible, just uninspired, when compared to the detail and vision of a production like Lord Of The Rings. The atmosphere and environs of the world of Barsoom, when compared to say, Avatar was flat and boring, and it did not have to be. (And this is coming from a person from Utah, where most of the exterior portions of the film were shot, and I LOVE our scenic wonders.) The landscape could have been built upon to make it far more fantastical and exotic, but the creators imagination faltered on that front. The acting was uneven from actor to actor, and the writing was similarly of mediocre caliber, scaled against something like Blade Runner. To me Taylor Kitsch looked more like another pulp hero in Conan The Barbarian, but without the Bad Mofo attitude. Lynn Collins was pretty enough, I suppose, but I would have rather had somebody with the exotic, feline face of Olivia Wilde, and the bangin' body of Heidi Klum in her prime. In any event, I can envision many other actors that could play the two lead characters far better. William Dafoe was fine for the voice of Tars Tarkas, however, but Dafoe can chew up scenery any day of the week. He's frickin' Dafoe, after all.<br /><br />Why do I make these comparisons to other films?<br /><br />Many reasons...<br /><br />But mostly because of the spendthrift production mindset and disrespect for the source material of the filmmakers.<br /><br />This film should never have cost this much. It was irresponsible and stupid on the part of Stanton and the production team, and they shot themselves in the foot, even if the film turned out halfway decent. But it is not fabulously awesome, mind-blowing, or jaw-dropping. Despite all the other glowing reviews on this site, the viewers of this film did not get the bang for their buck that production cost should have delivered.<br /><br />This film cost more than Avatar, for cryin' in the night!!! $250 plus bloody million!!!<br /><br />It should have never cost more than $160 million, even with reshoots. This should have been $120-100 million, if that. Films like [[ASIN:B00005JPLW 300]], [[ASIN:B007H7XJD4 Immortals]], and [[ASIN:B003RV43X4 Clash of the Titans]] were made for far less and had much more visual value put on screen. So disappointing.<br /><br />Add to that the shredding of the original Burroughs' texts, with only a general resemblance to [[ASIN:1598531654 A Princess of Mars]], and the film becomes almost insulting to diehard fans, who by the way, should have been the base the marketing and word of mouth this film should have been built on, but I digress.<br /><br />The general dearth of action that was the trademark of the book left me wanting more, and not in a good way. There was one scene where John Carter fights the Warhoons, and it was interspersed with redundant supposedly emotional flashbacks. The whole film, I was just waiting for him to cut loose, and show the John Carter I have grown to know and love, but no such luck.<br /><br />Oh, how I wish another studio and director had made this film. Please, please, please, New Line or Lionsgate, call Robert Rodriguez or Zack Snyder, or anybody who truly knows how to make action flicks effectively, and reboot this franchise!!! I pray to the Pulp Fiction Gods across the Multiverse that this will someday be so!!!<br /><br />For those of you who truly know the Barsoom books, I don't have to explain my dissatisfaction. This film was not how ERB penned his story, and yes, I understand that a film cannot be completely like a book, but it can be faithful, and congeal its essence, as in the case of Lord Of The Rings. Stanton did not do this, no matter how much he protests to the contrary. Even if they had strayed too far from the texts, but made a masterful script, I could have accepted it, but they did not. The script was middling at best. It wasn't too complex, as some have asserted, but it did have gaping plot holes, and stilted language that was not delivered with gravitas and sincerity. There was little of ERB's voice, and if the prose of Shakespeare can be delivered intelligibly, Burroughs' definitely can as well.<br /><br />Many reviewers have said they have read and loved the books, and love the film, and that's fine, but have you read them lately? I read all 11 just before the opening of the film. I was unimpressed with the conceptualization of the original stories. And that is just disheartening.<br /><br />As a person who has read the complete set of all 11 Barsoom books over 20 times, and the first trilogy over 40, I know of which I'm speaking. Over all, the film is a rollicking bit of fluff, with no depth. There is nothing wrong with that, but it could have been so much more. Some might say that pulpy fluff is what all Burroughs stories are in the first place, but I say poppycock to that! His stories would not have remained so influential if they did not strike a deep chord within many an artist's heart and mind.<br /><br />In conclusion, I am saddened by the combination of studio politics and myopic filmmaking that resulted in this film being just merely okay. If it had been done right, it would have been epic and classic and superlative beyond measure, but it wasn't. It was...okay. (sigh)<br /><br />With regret... 3 Stars.<br /><br />But that's just my opinion, and everybody's got one.s much. It was irresponsible and stupid on the part of Stanton and the production team, and they shot themselves in the foot, even if the film turned out halfway decent. But it is not fabulously awesome, mind-blowing, or jaw-dropping. Despite all the other glowing reviews on this site, the viewers of this film did not get the bang for their buck that production cost should have delivered.<br /><br />This film cost more than Avatar, for cryin' in the night!!! $250 plus bloody million!!!<br /><br />It should have never cost more than $160 million, even with reshoots. This should have been $120-100 million, if that. Films like [[ASIN:B00005JPLW 300]], [[ASIN:B007H7XJD4 Immortals]], and [[ASIN:B003RV43X4 Clash of the Titans]] were made for far less and had much more visual value put on screen. So disappointing.<br /><br />Add to that the shredding of the original Burroughs' texts, with only a general resemblance to [[ASIN:1598531654 A Princess of Mars]], and the film becomes almost insulting to diehard fans, who by the way, should have been the base the marketing and word of mouth this film should have been built on, but I digress.<br /><br />The general dearth of action that was the trademark of the book left me wanting more, and not in a good way. There was one scene where John Carter fights the Warhoons, and it was interspersed with redundant supposedly emotional flashbacks. The whole film, I was just waiting for him to cut loose, and show the John Carter I have grown to know and love, but no such luck.<br /><br />Oh, how I wish another studio and director had made this film. Please, please, please, New Line or Lionsgate, call Robert Rodriguez or Zack Snyder, or anybody who truly knows how to make action flicks effectively, and reboot this franchise!!! I pray to the Pulp Fiction Gods across the Multiverse that this will someday be so!!!<br /><br />For those of you who truly know the Barsoom books, I don't have to explain my dissatisfaction. This film was not how ERBpenned his story, and yes, I understand that a film cannot be completely like a book, but it can be faithful, and congeal its essence, as in the case of Lord Of The Rings. Stanton did not do this, no matter how much he protests to the contrary. Even if they had strayed too far from the texts, but made a masterful script, I could have accepted it, but they did not. The script was middling at best. It wasn't too complex, as some have asserted, but it did have gaping plot holes, and stilted language that was not delivered with gravitas and sincerity. There was little of ERB's voice, and if the prose of Shakespeare can be delivered intelligibly, Burroughs' definitely can as well.<br /><br />Many reviewers have said they have read and loved the books, and love the film, and that's fine, but have you read them lately? I read all 11 just before the opening of the film. I was unimpressed with the conceptualization of the original stories. And that is just disheartening.<br /><br />As a person who has read the complete set of all 11 Barsoom books over 20 times, and the first trilogy over 40, I know of which I'm speaking. Over all, the film is a rollicking bit of fluff, with no depth. There is nothing wrong with that, but it could have been so much more. Some might say that pulpy fluff is what all Burroughs stories are in the first place, but I say poppycock to that! His stories would not have remained so influential if they did not strike a deep chord within many an artist's heart and mind.<br /><br />In conclusion, I am saddened by the combination of studio politics and myopic filmmaking that resulted in this film being just merely okay. If it had been done right, it would have been epic and classic and superlative beyond measure, but it wasn't. It was...okay. (sigh)<br /><br />With regret... 3 Stars.<br /><br />But that's just my opinion, and everybody's got one.

B000002KO3|5.0|Recorded in 1980 but still sounds utterly contemporary and ahead of its time.  Talking Heads and Brian Eno fused the brain of David Byrne's (appropriately) neurotic/paranoid/shattered lyrical concerns and observations with the body and funky soul of Fela Kuti's hypnotic rhythms, arrangements and chord-changeless song structures, resulting in a record that can readily stand as not just an essential recording of the 20th century, but as a work of art.  It is no minor feat that it also happens to be very listenable and danceable, effortlessly avoiding the considerable potential pitfalls of pretension and dilettantism inherent in such an ambitious, cross-cultural endeavor.<br /><br />\\"Fear of Music\\" and \\"Speaking In Tongues\\" bookended this style for the Talking Heads, the former being darker and more closely related to their earlier art school style, and the latter being lighter and more commercial.  \\"Remain In Light\\" towers over the rest of their impressive catalog as their masterpiece, and provides a rock/new wave parallel, to my mind, of the modal exploration of \\"Kind of Blue\\".

This is far from true, not to say a lie. Per capita (PPP) income in Mexico (aka average Mexican) is: $15,100 (2011 est.) according to the CIA World Fact Book. Per capita (PPP) income in the USA (aka average \\"American\\") is: $48,100 (2011 est.) according to the same CIA World Fact Book. THAT IS 3.2 TIMES MORE, NOT 7. There are countries like Qatar and the United Arab Emirates that are far from democratic which have a higher PPP than that of the USA. [...]<br /><br />2-\\"Mexican average annual household income is around $5,000\\", WRONG AGAIN. The average income of a Mexican family of four is $60,400 ($15,100 x 4). In the same manner an average income of a family of four in the US $192,400 ($48,100 x 4). If you have a family of four and make less, now you know that you may be above the median, but certainly you are below average. That is 95% of the population of the USA is below average. There is a BIG difference between the average and the median household income. We learn that in high school.<br />Furthermore, the median worker in Mexico may be paid $5,000 a year, but on top of that his employee pays $2,500 for Social Security which is very similar to the American version, but also includes childcare and health insurance for all the household (no 25 year old limit, it includes all children, parents or grandparents that may qualify dependents).<br /><br />3-Life Expectancy. B00004TR2G|5.0|When John Williams wrote the score for Steven Spielberg's chilling film about a great white shark terrorizing a New England resort, he could not have done a better job than the ominous, 2-tone cello/bass theme that made viewers aware of what waited below the water's surface, even when it couldn't be seen. While Williams would outdo himself in composing such soundtrack masterpieces as the original 'Star Wars' trilogy, the 'Indiana Jones' trilogy, and 'Superman: The Movie', he nonetheless created a score here that is STILL remembered as one of the finest in cinematic history. The score was one major component that earned 'Jaws' such accolades at the time of its release.<br /><br />For the record, this is the full deal here: all the tracks originally written for 'Jaws' are presented here, and in chronological order, something a lot of movie soundtrack buffs appreciate. The 'Main Title' presented here is the true original one from the film, unlike the original release which used the orchestral/theme version of it. A second track, called 'Chrissie's Death' on the original release, was merged with the main title for this recording which I think improves the continuity. 'The Pier Incident', in which 2 men, using a man's wife's holiday roast as bait, draw the shark into shore, uses mostly strings to portray the action in the film, changing in pitch from a higher violin to the low cello/bass for depicting the shark as it closes in on one of the men after ripping out the pier. The cello/bass theme tapers off as the man makes it to shore and the pier washes up next to him. 'Shark Attack', a previously unreleased (and unused) cue was a fantastic piece of work, using both brass & strings to depict the shark's motif. This track is actually heard in the 'Jaws Unleashed' video game and its advertising. 'Into the Estuary', also previously unreleased, uses strings quite heavily to build a panic as Brody runs toward the estuary after a woman spots the shark & starts calling; the tone changes to a darker, lower one as Brody is seen running along the shore followed by a mob of people, then slows abruptly as the shark knocks over a rowboat, turning the occupant into the water and then attacking him. A chilling string & harp motif appears, cutting then to a French Horn cue as the shark first makes a threatening pass at Mike Brody and then heads back out into the open sea, its telltale fin visible to the people on the beach. 'Blown to Bits' details the shark's demise at the hands of Brody, using a string/harp motif as the shark's body descends into the depths after Brody blows it up with a compressed air tank. The original end titles are also included here - I seem to remember they were omitted or edited on the original release.<br /><br />This was LONG overdue for a complete release; probably due to the restrictions of vinyl, a restriction no longer applicable; in addition to the decreased soundtrack market which led to limited release of soundtrack music (if they were released at all), kept this from being released until now. Bravo for releasing this great score in its entirety!changes to a darker, lower one as Brody is seen running along the shore followed by a mob of people, then slows abruptly as the shark knocks over a rowboat, turning the occupant into the water and then attacking him. A chilling string & harp motif appears, cutting then to a French Horn cue as the shark first makes a threatening pass at Mike Brody and then heads back out into the open sea, its telltale fin visible to the people on the beach. 'Blown to Bits' details the shark's demise at the hands of Brody, using a string/harp motif as the shark's body descends into the depths after Brody blows it up with a compressed air tank. The original end titles are also included here - I seem to remember they were omitted or edited on the original release.<br /><br />This was LONG overdue for a complete release; probably due to the restrictions of vinyl, a restriction no longer applicable; in addition to the decreased soundtrack market which led to limited release of soundtrack music (if they were released at all), kept this from being released until now. Bravo for releasing this great score in its entirety!

And although the album begins and ends with self-serving, punchy tracks, it also flows through a series of more introspective songs.<br /><br />The first few tracks are catchy, but simply self-congratulatory. \\"Roman Holiday\\" feels a bit like Gary Numan recorded an album at a Mayan temple. It feels much like the locations it names. It excludes Manhattan from its list of New York City boroughs, and rhymes Hulk Hogan with Hoboken. Whereas Minaj used \\"Super Bass\\" to describe her ideal man, Bobby V and Lil Wayne dominate this song with their claims to sexual  superiority. At first glance Nicki, rap's strongest woman, submits to the masculinity of the genre in \\"Sex in the Lounge.\\" But it's important to keep in mind how Nicki appraises men in her first verse: \\"Though he packin' that muscle, I'm addicted to brain.\\" This line not only nuances her preference for well-dressed coke dealers outlined in \\"Super Bass,\\" but also gives hope to all those nerdy white guys who would trade their life savings for a night with the rapper.<br /><br />\\"Starships,\\" like \\"Roman Reloaded\\" was previously released as a single. You've probably already heard it played to death on the radio already, and for good reason. The song combines Nicki's edgy pout with a Katy Perry-esque chorus and techno musical interludes. This is a great song to crank while driving down PCH on a cool summer day.<br />\\"Pound the Alarm\\" is a straightforward dance song, incorporating now thoroughly mainstream dubstep elements, wherein Nicki once again asserts her reputation as \\"a bad bitch\\" capable of holding her liquor and dominating the dance floor.<br /><br />\\"Whip It,\\" far from a Willow Smith cover, feels like a Vengaboys track played in a Parisian night club. But ultimately, \\"Pink Friday ... Roman Reloaded\\" suffers from the same problem as \\"Pink Friday.\\" These albums are not thematically cohesive, but acoustically similar collections of singles punctuated by blocks of more lyrically complex tracks with disappointingly plain beats. 1451627289|5.0|I didn't know what to expect in 11/23/63 having never read much of King. I like complete novels, books that don't leave me hanging with implausibilities, overblown narration or loose threads everywhere. I realize after reading 11/23/63 why King is considered one of our greatest authors. I won't get into the plot of 11/23/63, you can read that for yourself on the description - which you probably already have. Instead let's talk about the writing: It is complete, it is beautiful, and it never leaves you hanging. The voice of the narrator of 11/23/63, Jake Epping, carries you through the story and you are not left asking why, or how, but rather carried along. It's a complete narrative. Everything is answered, everything is foreshadowed at just the right moment, and through King's brilliant prose you live in Jake Eppings shoes.<br /><br />This is NOT a political book. Yes, deep in the last 1/3 there is a little politics, more or less observations of a future, but it is not what you might think. I've grown up believing King to be - for lack of better terms - a liberal writer. Don't let this view, or any political view, of King get in your way though. There is no politics of any significance in 11/23/63. I think this is an important distinction to make because this is Kennedy we're talking about, and stopping his assassination. Instead King sticks solidly to the characters and the story and leaves politics to nothing more than casual observations that I doubt anyone of any stripe will take issue with.<br /><br />I give great credit to King for his research. This book is very, very well researched. It's both a history book and a beautiful work of historial fiction. King cites many authors on the subject of the assassination and Oswald, of all flavors, and I appreciated that. He didn't take it one way or another but kept it as accurate to history as you can with a story like this.<br /><br />If there's one drawback to 11/23/63 is that it is a typical King novel: long. It will take you sometime, but again, it's a beautifully written story that is neither complex or terse. You are literally carried gently through the story and the reader is never left hanging. Another small drawback is with the character of Al. I wish there had been more of him. I wish there was more to be learned from him. I think Al could have contributed much, much more to the story than King allowed, but again this is only a minor complaint.<br /><br />11/23/63 is one of the finest, most complete novels I have ever read.

B000002GQE|4.0|Days of Future Passed is a significant departure from the R&B style that defined the first Moody Blues album, 1965's The Magnificent Moodies. They reinvented their approach into a union of classical and rock music, resulting in a pioneering record of symphonic progressive rock that became their definitive sound.<br /><br />\\"Cold-hearted orb that rules the night,<br />Removes the colours from our sight.<br />Red is grey and yellow white,<br />But we decide which is right.<br />And which is an illusion\\"<br /><br />The album is conceptually centered around the sequential transition from morning to night, tracing the perceptive patterns of time from the bright states of awakening to the brooding reflections before drifting into sleep. Grand orchestral sections bridge the songs, which in turn are shaped by the moods of each thematic event. The structure of the album is elaborate but not complex; despite the expansive conceptual foundation and the rich instrumentation, they strive for and achieve a certain musical elegance that keeps the songs consistently engaging and lucid. In-between the overture of \\"Day Begins\\" and the album's theatrical finale, marked by Graeme Edge's dramatically recited poems, the band delivers an impressive collection of conceptually reflective songs, carefully arranged according to the emotional environment of each movement. When at their most joyful and lively, as in the animated rock 'n' roll of \\"Peak Hour\\" or the blissfully atmospheric melodies of \\"Forever Afternoon (Tuesday)\\", the enthusiasm never becomes naive or exaggerated, just as their most brooding moments never become universally morose. The driving mood rock of \\"The Sun Set (Twilight Time)\\" is their most effective portrayal of the harmonic synthesis between the two ends of the emotional spectrum, its contemplative tone provided a sense of forward momentum nicely setting the stage for \\"Nights in White Satin\\", the album's grand masterpiece of distant, passionate longing. Throughout thealbum, Mike Pinder's mellotron-produced melodic backdrops, the ephemeral flute melodies of Ray Thomas, and the band's penchant for invigorating multi-part vocal harmonies produce a consistently endearing and distinct effect, while countering the album's theatrical extravagance with fluid melodic constructions.<br /><br />\\"I can see it all<br />From this great height.<br />I can feel the sun<br />Slipping out of sight<br />And the world still goes on<br />Through the night.\\"<br /><br />The themes of childlike wonder and melancholic yearning, sometimes delivered in a lighthearted tone, are contextually grounded in spiritual exploration and cosmological enchantment, sonically manifested in the psychedelic atmospheres and daydreaming pop melodies. Day of Future Passed is an adventurous and inspired work, unique in the development of British symphonic rock of the time, demonstrating an exquisite combination of sounds in a vast thematic scope without overwhelming the genuine nature of their artistic perception.

B0059XTTW8|3.0|Viewers' reactions to this movie must depend, in large part, on their expectations and their history with the novel and '70s era BBC miniseries.  Le Carre may be one of the great British novelists of the last 50 years who simply happened to mine the vein of espionage fiction and was at the peak of his powers, and ideally suited to deal with the Cold War milieu, in the \\"Smiley Trilogy\\": Tinker Tailor, Smiley's People and The Honourable Schoolboy.  If you are a fan of the novel, and watch the movie making constant comparisons, you will probably be disappointed. Le Carre's fiction is challenging, layered and deeply perceptive, and the events are intricately interwoven.  A two hour movie simply cannot convey all the novel has to offer, omits and changes certain significant details, merges characters, and did not, for me, convey the deep ambiguities and analysis of betrayal at the heart of the novel. The movie also failed to create a great deal of suspence.<br />Likewise, a viewer who has recently seen the 1979 BBC miniseries starring Alec Guiness as George Smiley also will be disappointed. Guiness was terrific and managed to make of Smiley a much deeper and more complex character than does Gary Oldman in the time allotted to him. The cast of the miniseries also was brilliant, from Hywel Bennett as Ricki Tarr, through Ian Richardson, Bernard Hepton, Terence Rigby, Sian Phillips, Joss Ackland, Patrick Stewart, Ian Bannen, et al.  The miniseries was probably one of the best ever BBC productions and had the time (about 5.5 hours) to develop most all of the characters of the novel, explore its subtleties, and make sense of and explain the interconnectedness of the various strands of the plot that come together and allow Smiley to find the \\"mole.\\" Again, if you watch the movie comparing it constantly to the miniseries, I think you will be disappointed. There is a lot left out, many of the characters are barely developed, Anne, Smiley's wife, doesn't appear at all, except obliquely in a few scenes, and the sense of mystery and drama in the miniseries is much greater--you are literally on the edge of your seat in the scene in which the traitor is unmasked.<br />That being said, obviously, condensing the book to two hours must have been a daunting challenge, but the movie is still enjoyable and far from a failure. Gary Oldman is good as Smiley, but seems to play most of the movie in a passive state of shock.  Smiley is a damaged and conflicted man, beset by betrayal on all sides, but Oldman's protrayal is, for me, too strangely passive, and I don't think we get a feeling for what Smiley is all about.  Similarly, the other \\"suspects,\\" meaning the power elite at the Circus (British Intelligence), are well enough acted, but hardly developed at all. SPOILER ALERT  My main complaint has to do with one of the finer actors in the cast, Colin Firth, who plays Bill Haydon.  Haydon is supposed to be the \\"golden boy\\" of the Circus, the \\"laughing cavalier\\" whom the younger generation venerates and so talented and magnetic that, according to Prideaux (in the novel and miniseries) \\"you just can't judge Bill by ordinary standards.\\"  Unfortunately, we really don't see enough of Haydon to understand the nature and magnitude of his betrayal.  It would be unfair, however, not to note that John Hurt is excellent as Control.<br />I also have to wonder whether the movie will simply make sense to viewers who come unprepared. For example, we don't quite get the idea that the failed Hungarian gambit (why it has been changed from Czechoslovakia, I don't know) was Control's last ditch attempt to unmask the traitor, and I didn't find it clear in the movie how Tarr's final assignment was designed to flush out the traitor (very clear in the book and miniseries).  There are a host of other changes of which one could complain: the merging of Jerry Westerby and Sam Collins, the sexual orientation of Peter Guillam, the absence of Anne, the absence of Karla (instead of a scene between Smiley and Karla we get only Smiley's tale of their encounter).  --But of course I am inevitably comparing the movie with the other versions.<br />All in all, if you have some background with the book or the miniseries, I think that comparisons will be inevitable and the movie certainly has not improved on either version.  At the same time, as thin as it sometimes is, the movie is worth watching. The acting is solid, there is enough suspence to keep things interesting, and the atmosphere is effective.  At the least, perhaps it will encourage viewers to read the book or watch the miniseries, both of which are excellent.ene between Smiley and Karla we get only Smiley's tale of their encounter).  --But of course I am inevitably comparing the movie with the other versions.<br />All in all, if you have some background with the book or the miniseries, I think that comparisons will be inevitable and the movie certainly has not improved on either version. At the least, perhaps it will encourage viewers to read the book or watch the miniseries, both of which are excellent.

Let's just be honest here- it is.  \\"Pebbles Instrumental\\" is an instrumental that drags and drags and never features any memorable notes.  The main flute riff is especially insulting since it sounds like it consists of just a few notes played in the most generic way imaginable.  \\"Might-have-beens\\" is a short poem spoken by a downbeat and depressed Ian Anderson.  \\"Upper Sixth Loan Shark\\" is a short lullaby that's tolerable so at least it has that going for it.  One moment that's NOT tolerable is around the 43-second mark when Ian strains his voice.  Ugh!  You're not young anymore!
